{"Quotes": "[1] \"In steam cracking, ethylene is the preferred product, but the demand for propylene is growing faster than that for ethylene, especially in Western Europe [1]. Also, in the case of FCC, smaller amounts of propylene are produced.\"\n          [2] \"Dehydrogenations thermodynamic constraints limit alkane conversion; furthermore, a major problem is coking, which causes rapid catalyst deactivation.\"\n          [3] \"Therefore, the need exists for new technologies devoted to the production of specific olefins, which have none of the disadvantages characterizing dehydrogenation.\"\n          [4] \"In fact, the production of chemicals starting from alkanes by means of oxidative processes has been one of the most studied topics in recent decades.\"\n          [5] \"The effectiveness of the catalyst, that is the activity for alkane conversion and the selectivity to desired products, will ultimately determine the economic attractiveness of an alkane-based chemical process.\"\n          [6] \"The successful commercial technologies will be those that link catalyst performance characteristics with innovative process designs.\"\n fuel-grade feedstocks.\"\n          [8] \"In this case, conversely, the improved energy efficiency (ODH is exothermal, while dehydrogenation and cracking are endothermal), and process simplicity are effective incentives.\"\n          [9] \"A large number of reviews dealing with the ODH of light alkanes have been published since the early 1990s [421].\"\n          [10] \"In the present review, we growing demand for propylene, especially in Western Europe, and the production capacity that favors ethylene over propylene. Additionally, dehydrogenation processes are limited by thermodynamic constraints that restrict alkane conversion, and coking can cause rapid catalyst deactivation. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by offering a route to produce propylene directly from propane, which can potentially match the market demand more closely. The process is exothermal, which improves energy efficiency compared to endothermal dehydrogenation and cracking processes. However, ODHP also presents its own set of advantages and limitations. The use of oxygen as an oxidant in ODHP can lead to higher energy losses due to the difference in combustion heat between the feed and the product, resulting in higher fixed costs. Moreover, the production of CO2, a main by-product in alkane ODH, adds to the environmental and economic considerations.", "Doi": "10.1016/j.cattod.2007.05.009"},
{"Quotes": "", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, such as steam cracking and fluid catalytic cracking, include high energy consumption, significant greenhouse gas emissions, and the production of a wide range of co-products, which can dilute the efficiency of propylene production. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by offering a more direct route to propylene production with potentially lower energy requirements and fewer co-products. However, ODHP presents its own set of advantages and limitations, particularly in the context of oxidant selection. Using oxygen as an oxidant in ODHP can lead to higher propylene yields due to the direct conversion of propane to propylene and carbon dioxide. However, the use of oxygen also presents challenges, such as the risk of deep oxidation to carbon dioxide and the need for oxygen separation from air, which can be energy-intensive. The selection of oxygen as an oxidant must balance the advantages of higher propylene yields with the limitations of potential over-oxidation and the energy costs associated with oxygen production. The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, such as steam cracking and fluid catalytic cracking, include high energy consumption, significant greenhouse gas emissions, and the production of a wide range of co-products, which can dilute the yield of propylene. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by offering a more direct route to propylene production with potentially lower energy requirements and emissions. However, ODHP presents its own set of advantages and limitations, particularly in the context of oxidant selection. Using oxygen as an oxidant in ODHP can lead to higher propylene yields due to the direct conversion of propane to propylene and carbon dioxide. However, the use of oxygen also poses challenges, such as the risk of deep oxidation to carbon dioxide and the need for oxygen separation from air, which can be energy-intensive. In contrast, using nitrous oxide (N2O) as an oxidant can improve propene selectivity and reduce the risk of deep oxidation, but it may result in lower activity due to slower reoxidation of the catalyst. The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, such as steam cracking and fluid catalytic cracking, include high energy consumption, significant greenhouse gas emissions, and the requirement for large-scale infrastructure. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by offering a more energy-efficient and potentially cleaner alternative. However, ODHP presents its own set of advantages and limitations, particularly in the context of oxidant selection. Using oxygen as an oxidant in ODHP can lead to higher propylene yields due to its higher reactivity compared to other oxidants. However, oxygen also poses challenges, such as the risk of deep oxidation, resulting in the formation of carbon dioxide and water instead of the desired propylene. Oxygen's high reactivity can also lead to the rapid deactivation of catalysts, necessitating the development of robust and selective catalysts. The selection of the oxidant is crucial in ODHP, as it significantly influences the reaction pathway, product selectivity, and catalyst stability. Nitrous oxide (N2O) has been shown to be a promising alternative oxidant, offering higher propylene selectivity and lower catalyst deactivation rates compared to oxygen. However, the use of N2O also presents its own challenges, such as higher production costs and potential environmental concerns due to its greenhouse gas properties.", "Doi": "10.1016/j.jcat.2010.06.010"},

{"Quotes": "[1] \"The co-production of propylene from steam and fluidized crackers is anticipated to be insufficient to satisfy the rapidly growing demand1. Consequently, there is a need for the development of economic on-purpose production techniques to produce additional propylene.\"\n          [2] \"The direct dehydrogenation of propane (DDP) is ther- modynamically limited and is highly endothermic (ΔHr = 29.70 kcal/mol), requiring temperatures that may exceed 973 K for sig- nificant propylene yields2.\"\n          [3] \"In principle, the introduction of CO2 as a mild oxidant into the feed alters the dehydrogenation pathway by oxidizing the abstracted hydrogen from the alkane and conse- quently releasing the heat of reaction that reduces operating temperatures2,3.\"\n          [4] \"The presence of CO2 can also increase the equili- brium conversion of propane by consuming H2 through the reverse water gas shift reaction (RWGS), as seen in the thermo- dynamic calculations in Fig. 1a.\"\n          [5] \"Additionally, unlike regular oxi- dative dehydrogenation with molecular oxygen, CO2 as a mild oxidant suppresses over-oxidation and thus minimizes the pro- duction of carbon oxides.\"", "English": "Current propylene production methods, primarily through steam and fluidized crackers, are expected to be insufficient to meet the rapidly growing demand for propylene. This necessitates the development of more economic and purposeful production techniques. Direct dehydrogenation of propane (DDP) is thermodynamically limited and highly endothermic, requiring temperatures that may exceed 973 K for significant propylene yields. The introduction of CO2 as a mild oxidant in the feedstock can alter the dehydrogenation pathway by oxidizing the abstracted hydrogen from the alkane, releasing the heat of reaction and thus reducing operating temperatures. CO2 can also increase the equilibrium conversion of propane by consuming H2 through the reverse water gas shift reaction. Unlike regular oxidative dehydrogenation with molecular oxygen, CO2 as a mild oxidant suppresses over-oxidation, minimizing the production of carbon oxides.", "Doi": "10.1038/s41467-018-03793-w"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"Olefins can also be produced from dehydrogenation reactions of alkane feedstocks; however, simple dehydrogenation reactions suffer from numerous limitations that restrict their extensive applications. The limitations include possible side reactions, thermodynamic constraints on selectivity and conversion, the endothermic nature of the process, which necessitates a high energy input, and coke formation resulting in catalytic deactivation.\"\n          [2] \"One modification that may overcome many of these problems is oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH), in which an oxidant is added to the reaction feed of a thermal dehydrogenation unit, thereby initiating the reaction via the oxidant rather than high temperature alone.\"\n          [3] \"Despite the desirable exothermic nature and the limited coke formation, ODH based on oxygen is confronted with the challenge of over oxidation of olefins to CO and CO2, as well as the possibility of reaction runaway.\"\n          [4] \"To suppress the complete oxidation of alkane to COx to ensure a reasonable selectivity while maintaining good conversion of the ODH reactions, application of catalysts has been investigated, and many catalytic systems were found to be active for the process.\"\n          [5] \"Supported vanadium-based oxides and mixed-metal oxides are among the most extensively studied because of their high thermal stability, large surface areas, and the favored reducibility and basicity that promote the ODH activity and selectivity.\"\n          [6] \"Nevertheless, to date, none of these systems has shown product yields comparable to those obtained by the current industrial technologies of olefin production.\"", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, particularly through the dehydrogenation of propane, include side reactions, thermodynamic constraints on selectivity and conversion, the endothermic nature of the process requiring high energy input, and coke formation leading to catalytic deactivation. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by adding an oxidant to the reaction feed, initiating the reaction through the oxidant rather than solely relying on high temperatures. This modification can potentially overcome limitations such as endothermic nature and coke formation. However, using oxygen as an oxidant presents its own set of challenges, including the overoxidation of olefins to CO and CO2 and the possibility of reaction runaway. To mitigate these issues, catalysts have been investigated, with supported vanadium-based oxides and mixed-metal oxides being extensively studied due to their high thermal stability, large surface areas, and favorable reducibility and basicity. Despite these efforts, no catalytic system has yet achieved product yields comparable to current industrial technologies for olefin production.", "Doi": "10.1021/jp410750c"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"Propene (C3H6) is among the foundation feedstock for large volume commodities and specialty chemicals, such as cumene, acrylonitrile,propylene oxide and high-purity polypropylene, and its demand is expected to increase significantly in the near future.1 At present, its industrial production is based on non-oxidative processing of petroleum-based materials, via steam cracking, fluid-catalytic- cracking and catalytic dehydrogenation.2 As far as the catalytic dehydrogenation is concerned,commercial processes use Cr2O3/Al2O3 (18-20 Cr wt%; Catofin press) or Pt(<1 wt%),Sn (1-2 wt%)/Al2O3 (Oleflex process).3 Possible replacements with lower costs and sustainable alternatives is attracting a rising interest since many years, and the oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH) of propane appeared among the most promising, because of the higher energy efficiency (ODH is exothermic, while dehydrogenation and cracking are endothermic processes), and process simplicity.\"\n          [2] \"A review on the catalysts for propane ODH was compiled by Cavani et al. in 2007,2 who considered the performances in ODH of propane with O2 as oxidant of almost 100 catalysts, with different active phases,reported in literature over the 2000-2006 period, and concluded that the best propylene yields resulting from propane ODH (ca. 30%) were still far from being interesting for industrial implementation, as well as the information concerning catalyst lifetime appeared quite limited.\"\n          [3] \"Moreover, in that review the set-up of catalytic materials and/or reactor configurations capable of maintaining high selectivity to propylene under conditions leading to high propane conversion was addressed as main direction for future development.\"\n          [4] \"Later on, an initial yield in C3H6 as high as ca. 47%, decreasing to ca. 33% after 8 h on stream were reported for propane dehydrogenation over GaOx based catalysts.4 More recently, the kinetics for the ODH of C3H8 on VOx catalysts was reviewed by Carrero et al., and for the systems considered the combination of reported conversions and selectivities resulted in yields in C3H6 not exceeding 30%.5 Conversely, Ovsitser et al.,reported the attainment of a yield in propene of ca. 40% for the oxidative dehydrogenation of C3H8 under O2 lean-conditions over VOx/SiO2 catalysts, even a quite long reaction time.6 Similar results were obtained by these authors for the oxydative dehydrogenation of iso-C4H10 to iso-C4H8.7 In this respect, the attainment of high selectivity to olefins via ODH of alkanes is basically limited, from a chemical point of view, by the use of O2 as oxidant, because of the occurrence of the over- oxidation to carbon oxides of both fed paraffins and produced olefins.\"\n          [5] \"On such a basis, the use of CO2 as mild oxidant has attracted attention after the successful use in other partial oxidation reactions such as dry reforming8,9 and oxidative coupling of methane.10, 11 Although ODH of propane in the presence of CO2 is an endothermic process (H298K= + 166.6 kJ/mol), the addition of a small amount of oxygen can turn the process towards an exothermic character, and additional positive effects towards the catalyst coking might be expected.\"", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, which are based on non-oxidative processing of petroleum-based materials, include high costs and sustainability issues. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by offering higher energy efficiency, as it is an exothermic process, unlike dehydrogenation and cracking which are endothermic. ODHP also presents process simplicity as an advantage. However, ODHP presents its own set of challenges, particularly in the context of oxidant selection. The use of oxygen as an oxidant can lead to over-oxidation to carbon oxides of both the fed paraffins and produced olefins, limiting the selectivity to olefins. The use of CO2 as a mild oxidant has been proposed to mitigate this issue, as it can turn the process towards an exothermic character and potentially have positive effects on catalyst coking.", "Doi": "10.1039/C5CY00998G"},
{"Quotes": "", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, such as steam cracking and fluid catalytic cracking, include high energy consumption, complex processes, and the production of by-products. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by offering a more energy-efficient and direct route to propylene. However, ODHP presents its own set of advantages and limitations, particularly in the context of oxidant selection. Using oxygen as an oxidant in ODHP can lead to deep oxidation reactions, resulting in the formation of CO and CO2, which reduces the selectivity to propylene. Oxygen also poses safety concerns due to its reactivity with hydrocarbons, necessitating the use of diluents and careful control of reaction conditions to prevent runaway reactions and ensure process safety. The specific challenges associated with using oxygen include the need for oxygen separation from air, which increases the cost of the process, and the requirement for precise oxygen dosing to avoid over-oxidation.", "Doi": "10.1134/S0023158418060010"},

{"Quotes": "[1] \"Most of the current propylene production is performed by steam cracking (SC) and fluidic catalytic cracking (FCC).1 However, in both the SC and FCC processes, propylene is produced as a by-product and the selectivity and yields are always low.2 The availability of ethane as a major component of natural gas at natural gas prices has attracted many ethylene producers to utilize ethane instead of naphtha as the feedstock in steam crackers. As a result, the ratio of propylene/ethylene produced from steam crackers continues to decline. At the same time, driven by the expanding polypropylene market, the demand ratio of propylene/ethylene is increasing. For these reasons, it becomes increasingly important to develop on-purpose propylene production technologies that offer higher propylene/ethylene production ratios.1 Propane dehydrogenation has long been thought to be an ideal route for propylene production because of the theoretical high propylene selectivity and low capital investment, as well as the independence of the propylene production cost to naphtha price.\"\n          [2] \"Propane dehydrogenation is limited by thermodynamics, so that very high reaction temperatures on the order of 1000K are required to give a high single-pass propane conversion.2,4 Hence, the separation of the resulting hydrogen and unreacted propane from propylene becomes an issue.\"\n          [3] \"Theoretically, introducing an oxidant can drive the reaction to be more thermodynamically favorable. However, the highest propylene yield reported for propane oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH) is only about 30%, owing mainly to the consecutive oxidation of propylene to oxygenated products.2,5 The oxidative dehydrogenation pathways to produce diolefins and aromatics suffer from the same issue.\"\n          [4] \"Although many different catalysts, promoters and alternative oxidants have been studied,2,5,6 a commercially viable breakthrough has yet to be achieved.\"\n          [5] \"Among the halogens, F2 is inappropriate because of its high reactivity and the unfavorable thermodynamics for its use in a halogenationdehydrohalogenation process. Similarly, Cl2 reactions with alkanes are always fast and non-selective, and under some conditions an explosion may occur.9 I2 reacts selectively, but relatively slowly, with propane at low temperatures to produce propyl iodides and propylene. High propylene yields can be achieved with stoichiometric I2 and propane feeding.10 However, this reaction is only thermodynamically favorable at high temperatures (FigureS1). Furthermore, as a stoichiometric reaction of I2 with propane is needed, the recycling efficiency of the relatively expensive I2 greatly limits the potential industrial application of this process.11\"\n          [6] \"Compared to chlorine and iodine, reactions of bromine with propane are more feasible. However, to resolve the polyhalogenation selectivity issue in gaseous alkane bromination processes, a higher propane to bromine ratio is required to avoid the formation of 2,2-C3H6Br2.7 Consequently, single-pass propane conversion, using present technology, has limited utility.\"", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, such as steam cracking (SC) and fluidic catalytic cracking (FCC), include low selectivity and yields for propylene, as it is produced as a by-product. The increasing use of ethane in steam crackers has led to a decline in the propylene/ethylene production ratio, while the demand for propylene is increasing due to the expanding polypropylene market. Propane dehydrogenation is limited by thermodynamics, requiring very high reaction temperatures for high single-pass propane conversion, which complicates the separation of hydrogen and unreacted propane from propylene. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by potentially offering higher propylene yields and selectivity, but it is limited by the consecutive oxidation of propylene to oxygenated products, resulting in a maximum reported yield of about 30%. The selection of oxidants is crucial, as F2 is inappropriate due to its high reactivity and unfavorable thermodynamics, Cl2 reactions are fast and non-selective, and I2 reactions are selective but slow and only thermodynamically favorable at high temperatures. Bromine reactions with propane are more feasible, but resolving the polyhalogenation selectivity issue requires a higher propane to bromine ratio, limiting single-pass propane conversion.", "Doi": "10.1002/cctc.201200913"},
{"Quotes": "", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, such as steam cracking and fluid catalytic cracking, are their high energy consumption and the requirement for large-scale centralized plants, which limits the utilization of natural gas reserves in remote areas. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by offering a potentially more energy-efficient and decentralized process. However, ODHP presents its own set of advantages and limitations, particularly in the context of oxidant selection. Using oxygen as an oxidant can lead to side reactions such as cracking and combustion, which can limit the selectivity and yield of propylene. The specific challenges associated with using oxygen include the need for catalysts that can efficiently activate propane under mild conditions while suppressing over-oxidation and side reactions, control selectivity, as well as the management of the exothermic nature of the reaction to prevent overheating and ensure safety. {"Quotes": "Propane oxydehydrogenation combining high propane conversion and high propylene selectivity has proved an elusive goal, and propane oxydehydrogenation as a route to propylene appears to be far from realizing its commercialization potential. One difficulty is that propylene is more easily oxidized than is propane, so that selectivity tends to decline rapidly with conversion. Another is that at temperatures above about 700 C, propane cracking becomes significant, and a variety of products other than propylene are produced.", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods include the difficulty in achieving both high propane conversion and high propylene selectivity simultaneously. Propylene is more susceptible to oxidation than propane, leading to a rapid decline in selectivity as conversion increases. At temperatures above 700 degrees Celsius, propane cracking becomes significant, resulting in the production of various products other than propylene. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by potentially offering higher conversion levels and selectivity to propylene compared to traditional dehydrogenation methods. However, ODHP presents its own set of challenges, particularly in maintaining selectivity as conversion increases, due to the higher reactivity of propylene compared to propane. Additionally, at higher temperatures, propane cracking becomes more significant, leading to the production of various by-products other than propylene. The selection of oxidant is also a critical factor, with oxygen presenting safety concerns and process upsets that need to be addressed.", "Doi": "10.1016/S0926-860X(01)00816-X"},

{"Quotes": "", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, such as steam cracking and fluid catalytic cracking (FCC), include high energy demands, low selectivity towards propylene, dwindling petroleum reserves, and rising oil prices. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by offering a more economical feedstock (propane) and potentially more efficient conversion technologies. However, ODHP presents its own set of advantages and limitations, particularly in the context of oxidant selection. The use of oxygen as an oxidant risks combusting the hydrocarbon reactants and products, making it challenging to control side reactions such as cracking and excessive oxidation. Mild oxidants like CO2 are preferred as they can improve catalyst activity without the risk of over-oxidation. Oxygen is an attractive oxidant for ODHP due to its ability to lower the reaction temperature to around 400°C and make the dehydrogenation reaction exothermic. However, the use of oxygen also introduces new challenges, such as the need to avoid overoxidation of the hydrocarbons in the feed to maintain high olefin selectivity. Carbon dioxide (CO2) is another oxidant that has been investigated for ODHP. CO2 can effectively consume hydrogen under dehydrogenation conditions, but it does not provide enough heat to compensate for the endothermic nature of the dehydrogenation reaction. The cofeeding of CO2 can also result in additional coke deposition due to the increased CO and decreased H2 partial pressures brought about by the reverse water-gas shift reaction. Moreover, CO2 competitively adsorbs on the surface, specifically on the basic sites of the catalyst materials, which can negatively affect their performance.", "Doi": "10.1021/cr5002436"},

{"Quotes": "[1] \"Propylene is an important raw material in the production of polypropylene,polyacrylonitrile, acrolein, and acrylic acid. The conventional manufacturing methods such as catalytic and steam cracking which produce propylene as a byproduct do not meet the everincreasing demand. Therefore, propane dehydrogenation (PDH) has received much attention (HeinritzAdrian et al., 2008):\"\n          [2] \"Direct catalytic dehydrogenation of hydrocarbons is characterized by low conversion per pass and low yields due to equilibrium limitation. The process is endothermic and the thermodynamic equilibrium is unfavorable (Olah and Molnar, 2003). Temperatures above 800 K are required for commercially feasible conversions. At these temperatures,undesirable side reactions limit the propylene yield and coke formation necessitates frequent catalyst regeneration.\"\n          [3] \"Catalytic oxidative dehydrogenation of propane by oxygen is an attractive alternative because it is an exothermic and nonthermodynamically limited reaction. However, the propylene selectivity of the reaction remains an unsolved problem owing to the over oxidation of propane (Xu et al., 2006).\"\n          [4] \"On the other hand, soft oxidants such as carbon dioxide appear as potential alternatives. The PDH in the presence of carbon dioxide (CO2PDH) is also of interest because it utilizes the greenhouse gas CO2 (Zangeneh et al., 2011).\"\n          [5] \"CO2 is a coke removing agent as well. Bartholomew (1984) reported that oxidizability in the gasification of coke was in the following order: O2(105) > H2O(3) > CO2(1) > H2(0.003), where numbers in parentheses indicate the relative ratios of the gasification with oxidants.\"\n          [6] \"The promoting effect of CO2 is explained either by consumption of the hydrogen in the reverse watergas shift reaction  RWGS (reaction 2), which shifts the equilibrium of propane dehydrogenation to completion (twostep pathway) or its participation in direct oxidation of propane to propylene (reaction 3) (onestep pathway):\"\n          [7] \"The oxide catalysts which are active in the nonoxidative dehydrogenation of propane are also active in the process with CO2. Among the investigated materials, those containing Cr or Ga were found to be the most active and selective (Michorczyk et al., 2011).\"\n          [8] \"Propane dehydrogenation with carbon dioxide is characterized by rapid catalyst deactivation due to coke formation. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Olefins can also be produced from dehydrogenation reactions of alkane feedstocks; however, simple dehydrogenation reactions suffer from numerous limitations that restrict their extensive applications. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Propene (C3H6) is among the foundation feedstock for large volume commodities and specialty chemicals, such as cumene, acrylonitrile,propylene oxide and high-purity polypropylene, wt%)/Al2O3 (Oleflex process).3 Possible replacements with lower costs and sustainable alternatives is attracting a rising interest since many years, and the oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH) of propane appeared among the most promising, because of the higher energy efficiency (ODH is exothermic, while dehydrogenation and cracking are endothermic processes), and process simplicity.\"\n          [2] phases,reported in literature over the 2000-2006 period, and concluded that the best propylene yields resulting from propane ODH (ca. 30%) were still far from being interesting for industrial {"Quotes": "No relevant quotes provided.", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, such as steam cracking and fluid catalytic cracking, include high energy consumption, low selectivity towards propylene, and reliance on dwindling petroleum reserves. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by offering a more energy-efficient process with higher selectivity for propylene production. However, ODHP presents its own set of advantages and limitations, particularly in the context of oxidant selection. Using oxygen as an oxidant can lead to overoxidation and the formation of undesired carbon oxides, while using molecular oxygen can result in higher selectivity but also higher costs. The specific challenges associated with using oxygen include the need for oxygen separation from air, which can be energy-intensive, and the risk of overoxidation, which can reduce the yield of propylene. Oxygen is a cheap and abundant oxidant, but its use can lead to overoxidation and the formation of undesired byproducts such as carbon oxides. The presence of oxygen can also cause hotspots in the catalyst bed, leading to temperature control issues. To mitigate these challenges, the development of suitable catalysts and reactor designs is crucial for the successful implementation of ODHP. The use of oxygen as an oxidant in ODHP presents its own set of advantages and limitations. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Most of the current propylene production is performed by steam cracking (SC) and fluidic catalytic cracking (FCC).1 However, in both the SC and FCC processes, propylene is produced as a by-product and the selectivity and yields are always low.2 The availability of ethane as a major component of natural gas at natural gas prices has attracted many ethylene producers to utilize ethane instead of naphtha as the feedstock in steam crackers. As a result, the ratio of propylene/ethylene produced from steam crackers continues to decline. At the same time, driven by the expanding polypropylene market, the demand ratio of propylene/ethylene is increasing. For these reasons, it becomes increasingly important to develop on-purpose propylene production technologies that offer higher propylene/ethylene production ratios.1 Propane dehydrogenation has long been thought to be an ideal route for propylene production because of the theoretical high propylene selectivity and low capital investment, as well as the independence of the propylene production cost to naphtha price.\"\n          [2] \"Propane dehydrogenation is limited by thermodynamics, so that very high reaction temperatures on the order of 1000K are required to give a high single-pass propane conversion.2,4 Hence, the separation of the resulting hydrogen and unreacted propane from propylene becomes an issue.\"\n          [3] \"Theoretically, introducing an oxidant can drive the reaction to be more thermodynamically favorable. Similarly, Cl2 reactions with alkanes are always fast and non-selective, and under some conditions an explosion may occur.9 I2 reacts selectively, but relatively slowly, with propane at low temperatures to produce propyl iodides and propylene. {"Quotes": "", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, such as steam cracking and fluid catalytic cracking (FCC), include high energy demands, low selectivity towards propylene, dwindling petroleum reserves, and rising oil prices. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by offering a more economical feedstock (propane) and potentially more efficient conversion technologies. However, ODHP presents its own set of advantages and limitations, particularly in the context of oxidant selection. The use of oxygen as an oxidant risks combusting the hydrocarbon reactants and products, making it challenging to control side reactions such as cracking and excessive oxidation. Mild oxidants like CO2 are preferred as they can improve catalyst activity without the risk of over-oxidation. Oxygen is an attractive oxidant for ODHP due to its ability to lower the reaction temperature to around 400°C and make the dehydrogenation reaction exothermic. However, the use of oxygen also introduces new challenges, such as the need to avoid overoxidation of the hydrocarbons in the feed to maintain high olefin selectivity. Carbon dioxide (CO2) is another oxidant that has been investigated for ODHP. CO2 can effectively consume hydrogen under dehydrogenation conditions, but it does not provide enough heat to compensate for the endothermic nature of the dehydrogenation reaction. The cofeeding of CO2 can also result in additional coke deposition due to the increased CO and decreased H2 partial pressures brought about by the reverse water-gas shift reaction. Moreover, CO2 competitively adsorbs on the surface, specifically on the basic sites of the catalyst materials, which can negatively affect their performance.", "Doi": "10.1021/cr5002436"},

However, oxygen also poses challenges, such as the risk of deep oxidation, resulting in the formation of carbon dioxide and water instead of the desired propylene. Oxygen's high reactivity can also lead to the rapid deactivation of catalysts, necessitating the development of robust and selective catalysts. The selection of the oxidant is crucial in ODHP, as it significantly influences the reaction pathway, product selectivity, and catalyst stability. Nitrous oxide (N2O) has been shown to be a promising alternative oxidant, offering higher propylene selectivity and lower catalyst deactivation rates compared to oxygen. However, the use of N2O also presents its own challenges, such as higher production costs and potential environmental concerns due to its greenhouse gas properties.", "Doi": "10.1016/j.jcat.2010.06.010"},

{"Quotes": "[1] \"Olefins can also be produced from dehydrogenation reactions of alkane feedstocks; however, simple dehydrogenation reactions suffer from numerous limitations that restrict their extensive applications. The limitations include possible side reactions, thermodynamic constraints on selectivity and conversion, the endothermic nature of the process, which necessitates a high energy input, and coke formation resulting in catalytic deactivation.\"\n          [2] \"One modification that may overcome many of these problems is oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH), in which an oxidant is added to the reaction feed of a thermal dehydrogenation unit, thereby initiating the reaction via the oxidant rather than high temperature alone.\"\n          [3] \"Despite the desirable exothermic nature and the limited coke formation, ODH based on oxygen is confronted with the challenge of over oxidation of olefins to CO and CO2, as well as the possibility of reaction runaway.\"\n          [4] \"To suppress the complete oxidation of alkane to COx to ensure a reasonable selectivity while maintaining good conversion of the ODH reactions, application of catalysts has been investigated, and many catalytic systems were found to be active for the process.\"\n          [5] \"Supported vanadium-based oxides and mixed-metal oxides are among the most extensively studied because of their high thermal stability, large surface areas, and the favored reducibility and basicity that promote the ODH activity and selectivity.\"\n          [6] \"Nevertheless, to date, none of these systems has shown product yields comparable to those obtained by the current industrial technologies of olefin production.\"", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, particularly through the dehydrogenation of propane, include side reactions, thermodynamic constraints on selectivity and conversion, the endothermic nature of the process requiring high energy input, and coke formation leading to catalytic deactivation. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Propene (C3H6) is among the foundation feedstock for large volume commodities and specialty chemicals, such as cumene, acrylonitrile,propylene oxide and high-purity polypropylene, wt%)/Al2O3 (Oleflex process).3 Possible replacements with lower costs and sustainable alternatives is attracting a rising interest since many years, and the oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH) of propane appeared among the most promising, because of the higher energy efficiency (ODH is exothermic, while dehydrogenation and cracking are endothermic processes), and process simplicity.\"\n          [2] {"Quotes": "[1] \"Most of the current propylene production is performed by steam cracking (SC) and fluidic catalytic cracking (FCC).1 However, in both the SC and FCC processes, propylene is produced as a by-product and the selectivity and yields are always low.2 The availability of ethane as a major component of natural gas at natural gas prices has attracted many ethylene producers to utilize ethane instead of naphtha as the feedstock in steam crackers. As a result, the ratio of propylene/ethylene produced from steam crackers continues to decline. At the same time, driven by the expanding polypropylene market, the demand ratio of propylene/ethylene is increasing. For these reasons, it becomes increasingly important to develop on-purpose propylene production technologies that offer higher propylene/ethylene production ratios.1 Propane dehydrogenation has long been thought to be an ideal route for propylene production because of the theoretical high propylene selectivity and low capital investment, as well as the independence of the propylene production cost to naphtha price.\"\n          [2] \"Propane dehydrogenation is limited by thermodynamics, so that very high reaction temperatures on the order of 1000K are required to give a high single-pass propane conversion.2,4 Hence, the separation of the resulting hydrogen and unreacted propane from propylene becomes an issue.\"\n          [3] \"Theoretically, introducing an oxidant can drive the reaction to be more thermodynamically favorable. However, the highest propylene yield reported for propane oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH) is only about 30%, owing mainly to the consecutive oxidation of propylene to oxygenated products.2,5 The oxidative dehydrogenation pathways to produce diolefins and aromatics suffer from the same issue.\"\n          [4] \"Although many different catalysts, promoters and alternative oxidants have been studied,2,5,6 a commercially viable breakthrough has yet to be achieved.\"\n          [5] \"Among the halogens, F2 is inappropriate because of its high reactivity and the unfavorable thermodynamics for its use in a halogenationdehydrohalogenation process. Similarly, Cl2 reactions with alkanes are always fast and non-selective, and under some conditions an explosion may occur.9 I2 reacts selectively, but relatively slowly, with propane at low temperatures to produce propyl iodides and propylene. High propylene yields can be achieved with stoichiometric I2 and propane feeding.10 However, this reaction is only thermodynamically favorable at high temperatures (FigureS1). Furthermore, as a stoichiometric reaction of I2 with propane is needed, the recycling efficiency of the relatively expensive I2 greatly limits the potential industrial application of this process.11\"\n          [6] \"Compared to chlorine and iodine, reactions of bromine with propane are more feasible. {"Quotes": "", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, such as steam cracking and fluid catalytic cracking (FCC), include high energy demands, low selectivity towards propylene, dwindling petroleum reserves, and rising oil prices. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by offering a more economical feedstock (propane) and potentially more efficient conversion technologies. However, ODHP presents its own set of advantages and limitations, particularly in the context of oxidant selection. The use of oxygen as an oxidant risks combusting the hydrocarbon reactants and products, making it challenging to control side reactions such as cracking and excessive oxidation. Mild oxidants like CO2 are preferred as they can improve catalyst activity without the risk of over-oxidation. Oxygen is an attractive oxidant for ODHP due to its ability to lower the reaction temperature to around 400°C and make the dehydrogenation reaction exothermic. However, the use of oxygen also introduces new challenges, such as the need to avoid overoxidation of the hydrocarbons in the feed to maintain high olefin selectivity. Carbon dioxide (CO2) is another oxidant that has been investigated for ODHP. CO2 can effectively consume hydrogen under dehydrogenation conditions, but it does not provide enough heat to compensate for the endothermic nature of the dehydrogenation reaction. The cofeeding of CO2 can also result in additional coke deposition due to the increased CO and decreased H2 partial pressures brought about by the reverse water-gas shift reaction. Moreover, CO2 competitively adsorbs on the surface, specifically on the basic sites of the catalyst materials, which can negatively affect their performance.", "Doi": "10.1021/cr5002436"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"Propane direct dehydrogenation (DH) as well as oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH) have attracted worldwide academic and industrial interests, but both suffer from limitations that have propylene production methods, such as fluid catalytic cracking (FCC) and steam cracking, have limitations, including high operating temperatures, high costs of precious metal catalysts, and catalyst {"Quotes": "[1] \"The demand for olefins, especially propylene, is expected to increase significantly in the near future. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Propylene is an important raw material in the production of polypropylene,polyacrylonitrile, acrolein, and acrylic acid. The conventional manufacturing methods such as catalytic and steam cracking which produce propylene as a byproduct do not meet the everincreasing demand. Therefore, propane dehydrogenation (PDH) has received much attention (HeinritzAdrian et al., 2008):\"\n          [2] \"Direct catalytic dehydrogenation of hydrocarbons is characterized by low conversion per pass and low yields due to equilibrium limitation. The process is endothermic and the thermodynamic equilibrium is unfavorable (Olah and Molnar, 2003). Temperatures above 800 K are required for commercially feasible conversions. At these temperatures,undesirable side reactions limit the propylene yield and coke formation necessitates frequent catalyst regeneration.\"\n          [3] \"Catalytic oxidative dehydrogenation of propane by oxygen is an attractive alternative because it is an exothermic and nonthermodynamically limited reaction. However, the propylene selectivity of the reaction remains an unsolved problem owing to the over oxidation of propane (Xu et al., 2006).\"\n          [4] \"On the other hand, soft oxidants such as carbon dioxide appear as potential alternatives. The PDH in the presence of carbon dioxide (CO2PDH) is also of interest because it utilizes the greenhouse gas CO2 (Zangeneh et al., 2011).\"\n          [5] \"CO2 is a coke removing agent as well. Bartholomew (1984) reported that oxidizability in the gasification of coke was in the following order: O2(105) > H2O(3) > CO2(1) > H2(0.003), where numbers in parentheses indicate the relative ratios of the gasification with oxidants.\"\n          [6] \"The promoting effect of CO2 is explained either by consumption of the hydrogen in the reverse watergas shift reaction  RWGS (reaction 2), which shifts the equilibrium of propane dehydrogenation to completion (twostep pathway) or its participation in direct oxidation of propane to propylene (reaction 3) (onestep pathway):\"\n          [7] \"The oxide catalysts which are active in the nonoxidative dehydrogenation of propane are also active in the process with CO2. Many bulk and supported oxidebased materials containing active species, such as Cr (Michorczyk and Ogonowski, 2003; Michorczyk et al., 2008; Michorczyk et al., 2010; Michorczyk et al., 2011), Ga (Michorczyk et al., 2003; Xu et al., 2006), V (Takahara et al., 2005; Ogonowski and Skrzynska, 2008) and Fe (Michorczyk et al., 2004) have been studied as catalysts for dehydrogenation of propane in the presence of CO2. Among the investigated materials, those containing Cr or Ga were found to be the most active and selective (Michorczyk et al., 2011).\"\n          [8] \"Propane dehydrogenation with carbon dioxide is characterized by rapid catalyst deactivation due to coke formation. Ga2O3 showed drastic deactivation during a few hours on stream (Zheng et al., 2005). Besides coke formation, the reduction of Cr(VI) to Cr(III) is responsible for deactivation of chromium based catalysts (Zhang et al., 2011).\"", "English": "The conventional methods of producing propylene, such as catalytic and steam cracking, do not meet the increasing demand for propylene. Propane dehydrogenation (PDH) is receiving attention as an alternative method. Direct catalytic dehydrogenation of hydrocarbons has low conversion per pass and low yields due to equilibrium limitations. The process is endothermic and the thermodynamic equilibrium is unfavorable. Temperatures above 800 K are required for commercially feasible conversions. At these temperatures, undesirable side reactions limit the propylene yield and coke formation necessitates frequent catalyst regeneration. Catalytic oxidative dehydrogenation of propane by oxygen is an attractive alternative because it is an exothermic and nonthermodynamically limited reaction. However, the propylene selectivity of the reaction remains an unsolved problem owing to the over oxidation of propane. On the other hand, soft oxidants such as carbon dioxide appear as potential alternatives. The PDH in the presence of carbon dioxide (CO2PDH) is also of interest because it utilizes the greenhouse gas CO2. CO2 is a coke removing agent as well. The promoting effect of CO2 is explained either by consumption of the hydrogen in the reverse watergas shift reaction, which shifts the equilibrium of propane dehydrogenation to completion (twostep pathway) or its participation in direct oxidation of propane to propylene (onestep pathway). The oxide catalysts which are active in the nonoxidative dehydrogenation of propane are also active in the process with CO2. Propane dehydrogenation with carbon dioxide is characterized by rapid catalyst deactivation due to coke formation.", "Doi": "10.1080/00986445.2015.1017638"},

{"Quotes": "", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, such as steam cracking and fluid catalytic cracking, include high energy consumption, significant greenhouse gas emissions, and the production of a wide range of co-products, which can dilute the efficiency of propylene production. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by offering a more direct route to propylene production with potentially lower energy requirements and fewer co-products. However, ODHP presents its own set of advantages and limitations, particularly in the context of oxidant selection. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Olefins can also be produced from dehydrogenation reactions of alkane feedstocks; however, simple dehydrogenation reactions suffer from numerous limitations that restrict their extensive applications. The limitations include possible side reactions, thermodynamic constraints on selectivity and conversion, the endothermic nature of the process, which necessitates a high energy input, and coke formation resulting in catalytic deactivation.\"\n          [2] \"One modification that may overcome many of these problems is oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH), in which an oxidant is added to the reaction feed of a thermal dehydrogenation unit, thereby initiating the reaction via the oxidant rather than high temperature alone.\"\n          [3] \"Despite the desirable exothermic nature and the limited coke formation, ODH based on oxygen is confronted with the challenge of over oxidation of olefins to CO and CO2, as well as the possibility of reaction runaway.\"\n          [4] \"To suppress the complete oxidation of alkane to COx to ensure a reasonable selectivity while maintaining good conversion of the ODH reactions, application of catalysts has been investigated, and many catalytic systems were found to be active for the process.\"\n          [5] \"Supported vanadium-based oxides and mixed-metal oxides are among the most extensively studied because of their high thermal stability, large surface areas, and the favored reducibility and basicity that promote the ODH activity and selectivity.\"\n          [6] \"Nevertheless, to date, none of these systems has shown product yields comparable to those obtained by the current industrial technologies of olefin production.\"", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, particularly through the dehydrogenation of propane, include side reactions, thermodynamic constraints on selectivity and conversion, the endothermic nature of the process requiring high energy input, and coke formation leading to catalytic deactivation. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by adding an oxidant to the reaction feed, initiating the reaction through the oxidant rather than solely relying on high temperatures. This modification can potentially overcome limitations such as endothermic nature and coke formation. However, using oxygen as an oxidant presents its own set of challenges, including the overoxidation of olefins to CO and CO2 and the possibility of reaction runaway. To mitigate these issues, catalysts have been investigated, with supported vanadium-based oxides and mixed-metal oxides being extensively studied due to their high thermal stability, large surface areas, and favorable reducibility and basicity. Despite these efforts, no catalytic system has yet achieved product yields comparable to current industrial technologies for olefin production.", "Doi": "10.1021/jp410750c"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"Propene (C3H6) is among the foundation feedstock for large volume commodities and specialty chemicals, such as cumene, acrylonitrile,propylene oxide and high-purity polypropylene, and its demand is expected to increase significantly in the near future.1 At present, its industrial production is based on non-oxidative processing of petroleum-based materials, via steam cracking, fluid-catalytic- cracking and catalytic dehydrogenation.2 As far as the catalytic dehydrogenation is concerned,commercial processes use Cr2O3/Al2O3 (18-20 Cr wt%; Catofin press) or Pt(<1 wt%),Sn (1-2 wt%)/Al2O3 (Oleflex process).3 Possible replacements with lower costs and sustainable alternatives is attracting a rising interest since many years, and the oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH) of propane appeared among the most promising, because of the higher energy efficiency (ODH is exothermic, while dehydrogenation and cracking are endothermic processes), and process simplicity.\"\n          [2] \"A review on the catalysts for propane ODH was compiled by Cavani et al. in 2007,2 who considered the performances in ODH of propane with O2 as oxidant of almost 100 catalysts, with different active phases,reported in literature over the 2000-2006 period, and concluded that the best propylene yields resulting from propane ODH (ca. 30%) were still far from being interesting for industrial implementation, as well as the information concerning catalyst lifetime appeared quite limited.\"\n          [3] \"Moreover, in that review the set-up of catalytic materials and/or reactor configurations capable of maintaining high selectivity to propylene under conditions leading to high propane conversion was addressed as main direction for future development.\"\n          [4] \"Later on, an initial yield in C3H6 as high as ca. 47%, decreasing to ca. 33% after 8 h on stream were reported for propane dehydrogenation over GaOx based catalysts.4 More recently, the kinetics for the ODH of C3H8 on VOx catalysts was reviewed by Carrero et al., and for the systems considered the combination of reported conversions and selectivities resulted in yields in C3H6 not exceeding 30%.5 Conversely, Ovsitser et al.,reported the attainment of a yield in propene of ca. 40% for the oxidative dehydrogenation of C3H8 under O2 lean-conditions over VOx/SiO2 catalysts, even a quite long reaction time.6 Similar results were obtained by these authors for the oxydative dehydrogenation of iso-C4H10 to iso-C4H8.7 In this respect, the attainment of high selectivity to olefins via ODH of alkanes is basically limited, from a chemical point of view, by the use of O2 as oxidant, because of the occurrence of the over- oxidation to carbon oxides of both fed paraffins and produced olefins.\"\n          [5] \"On such a basis, the use of CO2 as mild oxidant has attracted attention after the successful use in other partial oxidation reactions such as dry reforming8,9 and oxidative coupling of methane.10, 11 Although ODH of propane in the presence of CO2 is an endothermic process (H298K= + 166.6 kJ/mol), the addition of a small amount of oxygen can turn the process towards an exothermic character, and additional positive effects towards the catalyst coking might be expected.\"", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, which are based on {"Quotes": "[1] \"Most of the current propylene production is performed by steam cracking (SC) and fluidic catalytic cracking (FCC).1 However, in both the SC and FCC processes, propylene is produced as a by-product and the selectivity and yields are always low.2 The availability of ethane as a major component of natural gas at natural gas prices has attracted many ethylene producers to utilize ethane instead of naphtha as the feedstock in steam crackers. As a result, the ratio of propylene/ethylene produced from steam crackers continues to decline. At the same time, driven by the expanding polypropylene market, the demand ratio of propylene/ethylene is increasing. For these reasons, it becomes increasingly important to develop on-purpose propylene production technologies that offer higher propylene/ethylene production ratios.1 Propane dehydrogenation has long been thought to be an ideal route for propylene production because of the theoretical high propylene selectivity and low capital investment, as well as the independence of the propylene production cost to naphtha price.\"\n          [2] \"Propane dehydrogenation is limited by thermodynamics, so that very high reaction temperatures on the order of 1000K are required to give a high single-pass propane conversion.2,4 Hence, the separation of the resulting hydrogen and unreacted propane from propylene becomes an issue.\"\n          [3] \"Theoretically, introducing an oxidant can drive the reaction to be more thermodynamically favorable. However, the highest propylene yield reported for propane oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH) is only about 30%, owing mainly to the consecutive oxidation of propylene to oxygenated products.2,5 The oxidative dehydrogenation pathways to produce diolefins and aromatics suffer from the same issue.\"\n          [4] \"Although many different catalysts, promoters and alternative oxidants have been studied,2,5,6 a commercially viable breakthrough has yet to be achieved.\"\n          [5] \"Among the halogens, F2 is inappropriate because of its high reactivity and the unfavorable thermodynamics for its use in a halogenationdehydrohalogenation process. Similarly, Cl2 reactions with alkanes are always fast and non-selective, and under some conditions an explosion may occur.9 I2 reacts selectively, but relatively slowly, with propane at low temperatures to produce propyl iodides and propylene. High propylene yields can be achieved with stoichiometric I2 and propane feeding.10 However, this reaction is only thermodynamically favorable at high temperatures (FigureS1). Furthermore, as a stoichiometric reaction of I2 with propane is needed, the recycling efficiency of the relatively expensive I2 greatly limits the potential industrial application of this process.11\"\n          [6] \"Compared to chlorine and iodine, reactions of bromine with propane are more feasible. However, to resolve the polyhalogenation selectivity issue in gaseous alkane bromination processes, a higher propane to bromine ratio is required to avoid the formation of 2,2-C3H6Br2.7 Consequently, single-pass propane conversion, using present technology, has limited utility.\"", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, such as steam cracking (SC) and fluidic catalytic cracking (FCC), include low selectivity and yields for propylene, as it is produced as a by-product. The increasing use of ethane in steam crackers has led to a decline in the propylene/ethylene production ratio, while the demand for propylene is increasing due to the expanding polypropylene market. Propane dehydrogenation is limited by thermodynamics, requiring very high reaction temperatures for high single-pass propane conversion, which complicates the separation of hydrogen and unreacted propane from propylene. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by potentially offering higher propylene yields and selectivity, but it is limited by the consecutive oxidation of propylene to oxygenated products, resulting in a maximum reported yield of about 30%. The selection of oxidants is crucial, as F2 is inappropriate due to its high reactivity and unfavorable thermodynamics, Cl2 reactions are fast and non-selective, and I2 reactions are selective but slow and only thermodynamically favorable at high temperatures. Bromine reactions with propane are more feasible, but resolving the polyhalogenation selectivity issue requires a higher propane to bromine ratio, limiting single-pass propane conversion.", "Doi": "10.1002/cctc.201200913"},
{"Quotes": "", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, such as steam cracking and fluid catalytic cracking, are their high energy consumption and the requirement for large-scale centralized plants, which limits the utilization of natural gas reserves in remote areas. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by offering a potentially more energy-efficient and decentralized process. However, ODHP presents its own set of advantages and limitations, particularly in the context of oxidant selection. Using oxygen as an oxidant can lead to side reactions such as cracking and combustion, which can limit the selectivity and yield of propylene. The specific challenges associated with using oxygen include the need for catalysts that can efficiently activate propane under mild conditions while suppressing over-oxidation and side reactions, control selectivity, as well as the management of the exothermic nature of the reaction to prevent overheating and ensure safety. The weaker CH and CC bonds in propane compared to ethane make selectivity control a greater challenge in ODHP.", "Doi": "10.1002/ente.201900622"},

{"Quotes": "Propane oxydehydrogenation combining high propane conversion and high propylene selectivity has proved an elusive goal, and propane oxydehydrogenation as a route to propylene appears to be far from realizing its commercialization potential. One difficulty is that propylene is more easily oxidized than is propane, so that selectivity tends to decline rapidly with conversion. Another is that at temperatures above about 700 C, propane cracking becomes significant, and a variety of products other than propylene are produced.", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods include the difficulty in achieving both high propane conversion and high propylene selectivity simultaneously. Propylene is more susceptible to oxidation than propane, leading to a rapid decline in selectivity as conversion increases. At temperatures above 700 degrees Celsius, propane cracking becomes significant, resulting in the production of various products other than propylene. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by potentially offering higher conversion levels and selectivity to propylene compared to traditional dehydrogenation methods. However, ODHP presents its own set of challenges, particularly in maintaining selectivity as conversion increases, due to the higher reactivity of propylene compared to propane. Additionally, at higher temperatures, propane cracking becomes more significant, leading to the production of various by-products other than propylene. The selection of oxidant is also a critical factor, with oxygen presenting safety concerns and process upsets that need to be addressed.", "Doi": "10.1016/S0926-860X(01)00816-X"},
{"Quotes": "", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, such as steam cracking and fluid catalytic cracking, include high energy consumption, high capital and operating costs, environmental issues due to greenhouse gas emissions, and the production of byproducts that reduce the overall yield of propylene. {"Quotes": "", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, such as steam cracking and fluid catalytic cracking (FCC), include high energy demands, low selectivity towards propylene, dwindling petroleum reserves, and rising oil prices. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by offering a more economical feedstock (propane) and potentially more efficient conversion technologies. However, ODHP presents its own set of advantages and limitations, particularly in the context of oxidant selection. The use of oxygen as an oxidant risks combusting the hydrocarbon reactants and products, making it challenging to control side reactions such as cracking and excessive oxidation. Mild oxidants like CO2 are preferred as they can improve catalyst activity without the risk of over-oxidation. Oxygen is an attractive oxidant for ODHP due to its ability to lower the reaction temperature to around 400°C and make the dehydrogenation reaction exothermic. However, the use of oxygen also introduces new challenges, such as the need to avoid overoxidation of the hydrocarbons in the feed to maintain high olefin selectivity. Carbon dioxide (CO2) is another oxidant that has been investigated for ODHP. CO2 can effectively consume hydrogen under dehydrogenation conditions, but it does not provide enough heat to compensate for the endothermic nature of the dehydrogenation reaction. The cofeeding of CO2 can also result in additional coke deposition due to the increased CO and decreased H2 partial pressures brought about by the reverse water-gas shift reaction. Moreover, CO2 competitively adsorbs on the surface, specifically on the basic sites of the catalyst materials, which can negatively affect their performance.", "Doi": "10.1021/cr5002436"},

{"Quotes": "[1] \"Propylene is an important raw material in the production of polypropylene,polyacrylonitrile, acrolein, and acrylic acid. The conventional manufacturing methods such as catalytic and steam cracking which produce propylene as a byproduct do not meet the everincreasing demand. Therefore, propane dehydrogenation (PDH) has received much attention (HeinritzAdrian et al., 2008):\"\n          [2] \"Direct catalytic dehydrogenation of hydrocarbons is characterized by low conversion per pass and low yields due to equilibrium limitation. The process is endothermic and the thermodynamic equilibrium is unfavorable (Olah and Molnar, 2003). Temperatures above 800 K are required for commercially feasible conversions. At these temperatures,undesirable side reactions limit the propylene yield and coke formation necessitates frequent catalyst regeneration.\"\n          [3] \"Catalytic oxidative dehydrogenation of propane by oxygen is an attractive alternative because it is an exothermic and nonthermodynamically limited reaction. However, the propylene selectivity of the reaction remains an unsolved problem owing to the over oxidation of propane (Xu et al., 2006).\"\n          [4] \"On the other hand, soft oxidants such as carbon dioxide appear as potential alternatives. The PDH in the presence of carbon dioxide (CO2PDH) is also of interest because it utilizes the greenhouse gas CO2 (Zangeneh et al., 2011).\"\n          [5] \"CO2 is a coke removing agent as well. Bartholomew (1984) reported that oxidizability in the gasification of coke was in the following order: O2(105) > H2O(3) > CO2(1) > H2(0.003), where numbers in parentheses indicate the relative ratios of the gasification with oxidants.\"\n          [6] \"The promoting effect of CO2 is explained either by consumption of the hydrogen in the reverse watergas shift reaction  RWGS (reaction 2), which shifts the equilibrium of propane dehydrogenation to completion (twostep pathway) or its participation in direct oxidation of propane to propylene (reaction 3) (onestep pathway):\"\n          [7] \"The oxide catalysts which are active in the nonoxidative dehydrogenation of propane are also active in the process with CO2. Many bulk and supported oxidebased materials containing active species, such as Cr (Michorczyk and Ogonowski, 2003; Michorczyk et al., 2008; Michorczyk et al., 2010; Michorczyk et al., 2011), Ga (Michorczyk et al., 2003; Xu et al., 2006), V (Takahara et al., 2005; Ogonowski and Skrzynska, 2008) and Fe (Michorczyk et al., 2004) have been studied as catalysts for dehydrogenation of propane in the presence of CO2. Among the investigated materials, those containing Cr or Ga were found to be the most active and selective (Michorczyk et al., 2011).\"\n          [8] \"Propane dehydrogenation with carbon dioxide is characterized by rapid catalyst deactivation due to coke formation. {"Quotes": "", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, such as steam cracking and fluid catalytic cracking, include high energy consumption, significant greenhouse gas emissions, and the production of a wide range of co-products, which can dilute the efficiency of propylene production. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by offering a more direct route to propylene production with potentially lower energy requirements and fewer co-products. However, ODHP presents its own set of advantages and limitations, particularly in the context of oxidant selection. However, the use of oxygen also presents challenges, such as the risk of deep oxidation to carbon dioxide and the need for oxygen separation from air, which can be energy-intensive. However, ODHP presents its own set of advantages and limitations, particularly in the context of oxidant selection. However, oxygen also poses challenges, such as the risk of deep oxidation, resulting in the formation of carbon dioxide and water instead of the desired propylene. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Olefins can also be produced from dehydrogenation reactions of alkane feedstocks; however, simple dehydrogenation reactions suffer from numerous limitations that restrict their extensive applications. The limitations include possible side reactions, thermodynamic constraints on selectivity and conversion, the endothermic nature of the process, which necessitates a high energy input, and coke formation resulting in catalytic deactivation.\"\n          [2] \"One modification that may overcome many of these problems is oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH), in which an oxidant is added to the reaction feed of a thermal dehydrogenation unit, thereby initiating the reaction via the oxidant rather than high temperature alone.\"\n          [3] \"Despite the desirable exothermic nature and the limited coke formation, ODH based on oxygen is confronted with the challenge of over oxidation of olefins to CO and CO2, as well as the possibility of reaction runaway.\"\n          [4] \"To suppress the complete oxidation of alkane to COx to ensure a reasonable selectivity while maintaining good conversion of the ODH reactions, application of catalysts has been investigated, and many catalytic systems were found to be active for the process.\"\n          [5] \"Supported vanadium-based oxides and mixed-metal oxides are among the most extensively studied because of their high thermal stability, large surface areas, and the favored reducibility and basicity that promote the ODH activity and selectivity.\"\n          [6] \"Nevertheless, to date, none of these systems has shown product yields comparable to those obtained by the current industrial technologies of olefin production.\"", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, particularly through the dehydrogenation of propane, include side reactions, thermodynamic constraints on selectivity and conversion, the endothermic nature of the process requiring high energy input, and coke formation leading to catalytic deactivation. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by adding an oxidant to the reaction feed, initiating the reaction through the oxidant rather than solely relying on high temperatures. This modification can potentially overcome limitations such as endothermic nature and coke formation. However, using oxygen as an oxidant presents its own set of challenges, including the overoxidation of olefins to CO and CO2 and the possibility of reaction runaway. To mitigate these issues, catalysts have been investigated, with supported vanadium-based oxides and mixed-metal oxides being extensively studied due to their high thermal stability, large surface areas, and favorable reducibility and basicity. Despite these efforts, no catalytic system has yet achieved product yields comparable to current industrial technologies for olefin production.", "Doi": "10.1021/jp410750c"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"Propene (C3H6) is among the foundation feedstock for large volume commodities and specialty chemicals, such as cumene, acrylonitrile,propylene oxide and high-purity polypropylene, wt%)/Al2O3 (Oleflex process).3 Possible replacements with lower costs and sustainable alternatives is attracting a rising interest since many years, and the oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH) of propane appeared among the most promising, because of the higher energy efficiency (ODH is exothermic, while dehydrogenation and cracking are endothermic processes), and process simplicity.\"\n          [2] {"Quotes": "[1] \"Most of the current propylene production is performed by steam cracking (SC) and fluidic catalytic cracking (FCC).1 However, in both the SC and FCC processes, propylene is produced as a by-product and the selectivity and yields are always low.2 The availability of ethane as a major component of natural gas at natural gas prices has attracted many ethylene producers to utilize ethane instead of naphtha as the feedstock in steam crackers. As a result, the ratio of propylene/ethylene produced from steam crackers continues to decline. At the same time, driven by the expanding polypropylene market, the demand ratio of propylene/ethylene is increasing. For these reasons, it becomes increasingly important to develop on-purpose propylene production technologies that offer higher propylene/ethylene production ratios.1 Propane dehydrogenation has long been thought to be an ideal route for propylene production because of the theoretical high propylene selectivity and low capital investment, as well as the independence of the propylene production cost to naphtha price.\"\n          [2] \"Propane dehydrogenation is limited by thermodynamics, so that very high reaction temperatures on the order of 1000K are required to give a high single-pass propane conversion.2,4 Hence, the separation of the resulting hydrogen and unreacted propane from propylene becomes an issue.\"\n          [3] \"Theoretically, introducing an oxidant can drive the reaction to be more thermodynamically favorable. However, the highest propylene yield reported for propane oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH) is only about 30%, owing mainly to the consecutive oxidation of propylene to oxygenated products.2,5 The oxidative dehydrogenation pathways to produce diolefins and aromatics suffer from the same issue.\"\n          [4] \"Although many different catalysts, promoters and alternative oxidants have been studied,2,5,6 a commercially viable breakthrough has yet to be achieved.\"\n          [5] \"Among the halogens, F2 is inappropriate because of its high reactivity and the unfavorable thermodynamics for its use in a halogenationdehydrohalogenation process. Similarly, Cl2 reactions with alkanes are always fast and non-selective, and under some conditions an explosion may occur.9 I2 reacts selectively, but relatively slowly, with propane at low temperatures to produce propyl iodides and propylene. High propylene yields can be achieved with stoichiometric I2 and propane feeding.10 However, this reaction is only thermodynamically favorable at high temperatures (FigureS1). Furthermore, as a stoichiometric reaction of I2 with propane is needed, the recycling efficiency of the relatively expensive I2 greatly limits the potential industrial application of this process.11\"\n          [6] \"Compared to chlorine and iodine, reactions of bromine with propane are more feasible. However, to resolve the polyhalogenation selectivity issue in gaseous alkane bromination processes, a higher propane to bromine ratio is required to avoid the formation of 2,2-C3H6Br2.7 Consequently, single-pass propane conversion, using present technology, has limited utility.\"", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, such as steam cracking (SC) and fluidic catalytic cracking (FCC), include low selectivity and yields for propylene, as it is produced as a by-product. Propane dehydrogenation is limited by thermodynamics, requiring very high reaction temperatures for high single-pass propane conversion, which complicates the separation of hydrogen and unreacted propane from propylene. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by potentially offering higher propylene yields and selectivity, but it is limited by the consecutive oxidation of propylene to oxygenated products, resulting in a maximum reported yield of about 30%. {"Quotes": "", "English": "The primary challenges in current propylene production methods, such as steam cracking and fluid catalytic cracking (FCC), include high energy demands, low selectivity towards propylene, dwindling petroleum reserves, and rising oil prices. Oxidative dehydrogenation of propane (ODHP) addresses these issues by offering a more economical feedstock (propane) and potentially more efficient conversion technologies. However, ODHP presents its own set of advantages and limitations, particularly in the context of oxidant selection. The use of oxygen as an oxidant risks combusting the hydrocarbon reactants and products, making it challenging to control side reactions such as cracking and excessive oxidation. Mild oxidants like CO2 are preferred as they can improve catalyst activity without the risk of over-oxidation. Oxygen is an attractive oxidant for ODHP due to its ability to lower the reaction temperature to around 400°C and make the dehydrogenation reaction exothermic. However, the use of oxygen also introduces new challenges, such as the need to avoid overoxidation of the hydrocarbons in the feed to maintain high olefin selectivity. Carbon dioxide (CO2) is another oxidant that has been investigated for ODHP. CO2 can effectively consume hydrogen under dehydrogenation conditions, but it does not provide enough heat to compensate for the endothermic nature of the dehydrogenation reaction. The cofeeding of CO2 can also result in additional coke deposition due to the increased CO and decreased H2 partial pressures brought about by the reverse water-gas shift reaction. Moreover, CO2 competitively adsorbs on the surface, specifically on the basic sites of the catalyst materials, which can negatively affect their performance.", "Doi": "10.1021/cr5002436"},
